  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_797b3501-c4a7-4651-835a-59140e0689cc'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9628532085676243824">Anthony Burgess, author of <i>A Clockwork Orange,</i> is regarded as one of the most original and daring writers in the English language. His work is illuminated by a dazzling imagination, by a gift for character and plot, by a talent for surprise. <br/><br/>In <i>Earthly Powers</i> Burgess created his masterpiece. At its center are two twentieth-century men who represent different kinds of power</span>
  <span id="freeText9628532085676243824" style="display:none">Anthony Burgess, author of <i>A Clockwork Orange,</i> is regarded as one of the most original and daring writers in the English language. His work is illuminated by a dazzling imagination, by a gift for character and plot, by a talent for surprise. <br /><br />In <i>Earthly Powers</i> Burgess created his masterpiece. At its center are two twentieth-century men who represent different kinds of power—Kenneth Toomey, eminent novelist, a man who has outlived his contemporaries to survive into honored, bitter, luxurious old age as a celebrity of dubious notoriety; and Don Carlo Campanati, a man of God, eventually beloved Pope, who rises through the Vatican as a shrewd manipulator to become the architect of church revolution and a candidate for sainthood. <br /><br />Through the lives of these two modern men Burgess explores the very essence of power. As each pursues his career—one to sainthood, one to wealthy exile—their relationship becomes the heart of a narrative that incorporates almost everyone of fame and distinction in the social, literary, and political life of America and Europe. This astonishing company is joined together by the art of a great novelist into an explosive and entertaining tour de force that will captivate fans of sweeping historic fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9628532085676243824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_7022305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7022305" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7022305?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7022305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4403095924545910001">Some people really like this big old thing. But it was yet another in the tedious catalogue of huge masculine overbearing egomaniacal penis novels about a Big Man like, say, <i>I the Supreme</i> or <i>Illywacker</i> or <i>Gould's Book of Fish</i> or <i>The Book of Evidence</i> or <i>Mein Kampf</i> - boy, there's a lot of em. And it's the egomaniac's voice who narrates it. So you volunteer to have the guy bending your inner ear for page after page and no break. Maybe some readers channel their inner masochist and lie back and wall</span>
  <span id="freeText4403095924545910001" style="display:none">Some people really like this big old thing. But it was yet another in the tedious catalogue of huge masculine overbearing egomaniacal penis novels about a Big Man like, say, <i>I the Supreme</i> or <i>Illywacker</i> or <i>Gould's Book of Fish</i> or <i>The Book of Evidence</i> or <i>Mein Kampf</i> - boy, there's a lot of em. And it's the egomaniac's voice who narrates it. So you volunteer to have the guy bending your inner ear for page after page and no break. Maybe some readers channel their inner masochist and lie back and wallow in the hurling of the testosterone. Not me. I chucked it at the wall quite quickly. I could hear its fans screeching and clawing each other in genuine horror. But really, wordsmithery and large braininess will not save a book from the wall-hurl. The tone of voice was like the clench of rat-claws on a biscuit tin lid and I chose not to have that particular voice jabbering and gibbering and mewling in my ear for 600 pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4403095924545910001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6843326-abby?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229751808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8623962868587032521">A monumental novel, recently back in print, that has stuck in my mind for thirty years as an all-time favorite but needed to be reread to remind me why. An octogenarian British writer, asked to attest to a miracle that will support canonization of a Pope, writes his memoirs, giving us a personal tour of the 20th-century through his life as a homosexual, lapsed Catholic, successful but mediocre writer, and exile. Examines morality, the nature of evil, the role of religious belief and more. Lingui</span>
  <span id="freeText8623962868587032521" style="display:none">A monumental novel, recently back in print, that has stuck in my mind for thirty years as an all-time favorite but needed to be reread to remind me why. An octogenarian British writer, asked to attest to a miracle that will support canonization of a Pope, writes his memoirs, giving us a personal tour of the 20th-century through his life as a homosexual, lapsed Catholic, successful but mediocre writer, and exile. Examines morality, the nature of evil, the role of religious belief and more. Linguistically playful, the novel features one of the best opening lines in literature, and is funny, painful, thought-provoking, entertaining, challenging and rewarding. Thoroughly magnificent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8623962868587032521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_994741949" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/994741949" />
    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/994741949?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitaker" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker">Whitaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=fiction-1950-and-after">fiction-1950-and-after</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=z_2014-read">z_2014-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer994741949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10468119791486694064">That two-star rating might be a little unfair. I actually had fun reading large chunks of the book. If all you're looking for is something entertaining to read, and you enjoy British snark and bitchiness, then this is the ticket. <br/><br/>As a work of literature, however, it fell far short. And since it purports to seriously discuss the problem of good and evil, I think that's a fair yardstick. This is not, at the end of the day, the novelistic equivalent of Monty Python. <br/><br/>The novel follows the life of Ke</span>
  <span id="freeText10468119791486694064" style="display:none">That two-star rating might be a little unfair. I actually had fun reading large chunks of the book. If all you're looking for is something entertaining to read, and you enjoy British snark and bitchiness, then this is the ticket. <br /><br />As a work of literature, however, it fell far short. And since it purports to seriously discuss the problem of good and evil, I think that's a fair yardstick. This is not, at the end of the day, the novelistic equivalent of Monty Python. <br /><br />The novel follows the life of Kenneth Toomey, eminent novelist and very closeted gay man. Through him, we also follow the life of his sister, Hortense Toomey, and her husband, as well as the lives of her husband's family, the most notable member of which, Don Carlo Campanati, eventually becomes the pope. This is no spoiler, by the way, as the novel starts towards the end of Toomey's life when he is asked to provide evidence to support Don Carlo's proposed canonisation, and then jumps back to the beginning to explain how we got to that stage. <br /><br />What is good and what is evil looms large as a theme. Good actions in the novel turn out to have disastrous evil results, while bad actions turn out to have good ones. So, for example, Toomey accidentally saves a man from being shot and later it turns out that the man is Heinrich Himmler, the architect of the Nazi genocide. In a related example, a Jewish novelist living in Austria sends his son into Toomey's care to save him from the Nazis. The son turns out to be a nasty, lazy thief. However, when Toomey finds stolen British passports among his loot, he realises that he can use one of the passports to try to get the novelist out of Austria. <br /><br />The whole thing reminded me of a Chinese folktale which goes like this: A poor man lived in a village with his family. One day, his only horse ran away and his neighbours bemoaned his misfortune. The man just shrugged his shoulders and kept silent. <br /><br />The next day, the mare came back. With her was a powerful stallion, which had been attracted to her. The man's neighbours congratulated him for his good luck. The man just shrugged his shoulders and kept silent. <br /><br />A week later, the man's son tried to ride the stallion and broke his leg. He became lame as a result. The man's neighbours bemoaned his misfortune, but once again the man simply shrugged his shoulders and kept silent. <br /><br />A month later, the Emperor started a war and all the young men from the village were rounded up to join the Imperial Army. The man's son was spared because of his lameness. The man's neighbours congratulated him, but as ever the man simply shrugged his shoulders and kept silent. <br /><br />One of the morals of the tale is that you can never tell whether something that happens is good luck or bad luck, so you just have to accept things as they happen with equanimity. <i>Earthly Powers</i> turns that philosophical question into one about good and evil. It might even have earned three or four stars on that basis as Burgess tells his story with a good dose of brio. <br /><br />However, and this is a huge however, Burgess ruins it all by bringing in the devil. To me, at least, once you actually bring the devil onstage, which he does not once but twice via two full-on exorcisms, then all question of good and evil goes out the window. The devil is the incarnation of Evil. When you have spinning heads and projectile vomit what is there to discuss? <br /><br />The question then becomes less philosophical but theological, and you get the obligatory discussions about, "How can God be all good and all powerful when he created Lucifer knowing that he would fall from grace? How can God allow the devil to continue to exist instead of just destroying him?" Well, the answers are put into Don Carlo's mouth as he gives the bog standard Catholic explanation of free will as to why. Unfortunately, the rest of the novel then starts to look like Burgess making his puppets jump through hoops to illustrate why this answer doesn't wash. <br /><br />The hero of the novel is eventually held up to be Toomey's elder brother, Tom, a nice little man who led a good quiet inoffensively uneventful life as a comedian who told not very funny, inoffensive jokes. We even have a skit about him playing a parent warning his children (named "Kenneth" and "Hortense") not to stick their hands into dark dangerous places. <br /><br />So, in the end, it turns out that Burgess's answer to the problem of good and evil is pretty much the same as the point of that old Chinese folktale whose <i>other</i> moral, by the way, is that we shouldn't try to change things because we can't tell whether the results of our actions will be good or bad. Best to just leave things alone. So, oppressed workers and Africans really shouldn't try changing their lot. And yes communism and black rights do get swipes in the novel as well. <br /><br />Well, if you're a rich white man, telling the rest of the world that they should just leave things as they are is all very convenient. Sorry, but that doesn't wash. Mix that in with the actual existence of the devil and what you get is a confused mess really. Two stars, despite the somewhat nasty laughs. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10468119791486694064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2393038981" />
    <a title="P.E." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68429005-p-e"><img alt="P.E." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543802303p2/68429005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2393038981?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P.E." class="user" itemprop="url" name="P.E." href="/user/show/68429005-p-e">P.E.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099468" href="/book/show/9994130-earthly-powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=play-on-words">play-on-words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=unusual-narrator">unusual-narrator</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=argot">argot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=ideology">ideology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=languages-and-idioms">languages-and-idioms</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=book-about-books">book-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=tragic">tragic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2393038981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6639478599478499052"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1563112512i/27826657._SY540_.jpg' height='400"' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>
  <b>Heritage album cover art</b>
</i><br/><br/><br/><b>1. MUSICAL ACCOMPANIMENT</b><br/><br/><a>href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6tZ_uwDlmPY"></a><br/><b>
  <i>Providence - GSYBE!</i>
</b><br/><br/><br/><b>2. THE STORY</b><br/><br/>Writer Kenneth Toomey, 81 y.o., is requested to write a biography on Carlo Campanati, that is, a relative, also the former pope. <br/>The background of this curious hagiography consists in the fate of family Toomey and family Campanati during the 20th century. <br/>Also, Ken Toomey is a homosexual with a knack for bonding with ill-suited partners. <br/><br/><br/><b>3. EARTHLY POWERS?</b><br/><br/>I am</span>
  <span id="freeText6639478599478499052" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1563112512i/27826657._SY540_.jpg" height='400"' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>
  <b>Heritage album cover art</b>
</i><br><br><br><b>1. MUSICAL ACCOMPANIMENT</b><br><br><a>href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6tZ_uwDlmPY"&gt;</a><br><b>
  <i>Providence - GSYBE!</i>
</b><br><br><br><b>2. THE STORY</b><br><br>Writer Kenneth Toomey, 81 y.o., is requested to write a biography on Carlo Campanati, that is, a relative, also the former pope. <br>The background of this curious hagiography consists in the fate of family Toomey and family Campanati during the 20th century. <br>Also, Ken Toomey is a homosexual with a knack for bonding with ill-suited partners. <br><br><br><b>3. EARTHLY POWERS?</b><br><br>I am still wondering about the title. What are these earthly powers, actually ? The State, occasionally denying your being yourself, spoliating and expropriating you once in a while? <br>Money, twisting the game of social relations?<br>Celebrity and the endless representation it entails?<br>Universal chance, making the world a giant roulette, be it spending an evening in the casino or attending papal election?<br>Reproduction, pitting generations against one another? <br>Cults, ethnical communities? Discord induced and fostered by nationalisms and movements claiming territories in the name of a given ethnical group (pangermanism, panafricanism)? <br>The will to reform, degrading itself into a series of subpar, warped realizations perverting a generous idea (Communism, ecumenical Catholicism...)?<br><br>Against what kind of <i>spiritual</i> powers, then, are these earthly powers in deadly conflict?<br>The moral establishment?<br>French-born Mrs Toomey's Catholic faith?<br>The Catholic Church, backing traditional order and a vision of world's unity of their own?<br>A world split in between those obeying and those in command, neverchanging places according to Toomey?<br>Val Wrigley's uprightness and purported righteousness in the forwarding of the Queer cause?<br>Domenico's dedication to writing music genuinely embodying his feelings about the absence of kindly Providence?<br>Noncommital Kenneth, the writer who never loved anybody but his great love in Malaya and his sister?<br>Umilta's renunciation to the outside world, wasting away her days in the convent?<br>Carlo's painstaking ascent to power, a willing, well-meaning orphan, fueling his relentless energy with pent-up carnal apetites towards his brother Domenico's wife? The man who won't acknowledge man's drive for evil and power over fellow men for itself?<br>Disinterested, selfless Tom Toomey?<br>Perhaps. Still, I guess there is more behind the multifarious forms assumed by power and men harnessing it.<br><br><br><b>4. A FAMILIAL TRAGEDY</b><br><br>The red line of this novel is likely to be the fall of two families : the Tommeys and the Campanatis.<br>Mr. and Mrs. Campanati, Rafaelle, Domenico, Umilta and Carlo.<br>Mr. and Mrs. Toomey, Tom, Kenneth, Hortense.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As Kenneth first meets Mr. Campanati, the latter bedridden and senile, Mrs Campanati is on her way to be diagnosed with cancer, later to kill herself in a thwarted murder attempt. Uncompromising Raffaele will get retribution from the Sicilian mafia in Chicago ; Domenico, infertile, an author of light music, forsaken ; cold Umilta, hiding herself from the world in the convent ; Carlo, abandonned by his parents, growing into a short-tempered <i>papa</i> of everybody he once meets.<br>Tom undergoes sickness and emotional misery, Kenneth goes into exile, Hortense never succeeds in emancipating herself from her family ties, yet outlives all her descendants. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><br><b>5. LANGUAGE AND NARRATION</b><br><br>This is a curious book.<br>As soon as you believe you have a frame, a specific form of a narration, a manner of progress - life under the war, life after the war, Kenneth's evolution as a writer,... - another plot thickens : comedy of errors, tragic flaws in the Campanati family,...<br>The narration shies away from progress and order in his narration and highlights the tiring process of reconstruction and filling underlying the whole book.<br><br>By means of a fiction endlessly pointed out, Anthony Burgess gives his text a paradoxical weight, relating fantasized events in his life, itself a prodigious novel. I would say the very fabric of narration in this book is wariness towards all discourses flaunting lifelikeness, all narrations boasting truth. <br><br>This is no coincidence is the novel is packed with press clippings, radio declarations and television broadcasts, conferences, letters. No coincidence if the last work by Domenico Campanati is an adaptation of unadaptable Ulysses. I believe that this book extols the power of the text and evidences the all too real power of language structures on societies and their rituals. Sometimes in the flesh when this search is embodied by John and Laura Campanati.<br><br>Hence the universal, <i>catholical</i> ambition of this book, a living craze to describe akin to that one witnesses in Ulysses, if a bit tamed.<br>Hence the reserve, and both regard and skepticism inhabiting Kenneth when he faces words, his refusal to content himself with mastering only one language, one narration.<br><br>Do you keep a diary? I do, so all of this is quite familiar to me. The carefulness towards the way you remember past events and the way you put them down.<br><br>-------------<br><br><b>6. LITERARY SIBLINGS</b><br><br>For their common extraordinary gambling scenes :<br>The Gambler - F.M. Dostoevsky<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12857.The_Gambler" title="The Gambler by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gambler</a><br><br>On the matter of the banality of evil: <br>The Kindly Ones - Jonathan Littell<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3755250.The_Kindly_Ones" title="The Kindly Ones by Jonathan Littell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Kindly Ones</a><br><br>Because of its unmitigated amazement before the strangeness of the world :<br>The Transmigration of Timothy Archer - Philip K. Dick<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106585.The_Transmigration_of_Timothy_Archer" title="The Transmigration of Timothy Archer by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Transmigration of Timothy Archer</a><br><br>For the novel way it deals with a monument of tradition, as the Campanati do in their trades :<br>The Book of Genesis - Robert Crumb<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6371553.The_Book_of_Genesis" title="The Book of Genesis by Robert Crumb" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Genesis</a><br><br>For its puns and games on point of views :<br>Exercises in Style - Raymond Queneau<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/319790.Exercises_in_Style" title="Exercises in Style by Raymond Queneau" rel="nofollow noopener">Exercises in Style</a><br><br>For the naughty pen and obvious pleasure in reading :<br>Ulysses - James Joyce<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6957527.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a><br><br><br>-------------------<br><br><br><br><i><b>Heritage album cover art</b></i><br><br><br><b>1. ACCOMPAGNEMENT</b><br><br><a>href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6tZ_uwDlmPY"&gt;</a><br><b><i>Providence - GSYBE!</i></b><br><br><br><b>2. L'HISTOIRE</b><br><br>L'écrivain Kenneth Toomey écrit une hagiographie étrange sur Carlo Campanati, défunt pape, avec pour toile de fond le destin de deux familles au 20ème siècle, la famille Toomey et la famille Campanati.<br>Je n'en dis pas plus pour les intéressés, spoilers annoncés plus bas.<br><br><br><b>3. EARTHLY POWERS, LES PUISSANCES DES TÉNÈBRES ?</b><br><br>Je me pose encore la question de la raison de ce titre. Que sont-ils, ces pouvoirs <i>terrestres</i> ? chtoniens (?), infernaux (?).<br><br>Les pouvoirs publics qui vous frustrent d'être ce que vous êtes en public, qui vous spolient, vous exproprient à l'occasion ? <br>L'argent qui fausse tout le jeu des relations aux autres ?<br>La célébrité et la représentation perpétuelle qui la suit ? <br>Le hasard universel qui fait du monde un grand tapis de jeu, qu'il s'agisse d'une soirée au casino ou d'une élection papale au Vatican ? <br>La gestation qui inscrit les parents et les enfants dans des générations différentes, à l'occasion hostiles et distants ? <br>La discorde induite par les nationalismes et les mouvements de revendication territoriale ? <br>La volonté réformatrice qui s'accomplissant se mue en une chaîne d'accomplissements qui dévoient l'idée généreuse à leur origine (Communisme, œcuménisme catholique) ? <br>Les sectes, les groupes d'appartenance et de sentiments d'appartenance nationale (pangermanisme, panafricanisme) ? <br><br>Et face à quelles forces <i>célestes</i> ?<br>L'<i>establishment</i> moral ?<br>La foi catholique de Madame Toomey, née française ?<br>L'Église qui défend les ordres traditionnels et une certaine vision de l'unité où le monde se divise entre ceux qui commandent et ceux qui obéissent, places destinées à ne jamais changer au Royaume-Uni à en croire Kenneth ? <br>La rectitude et l'intégrité affichés de Val Wrigley le champion improvisé de la cause queer ?<br>L'investissement, l'implication de Domenico dans l'écriture de pièces qui reflètent son sentiment réel sur l'injustice du monde, sur l'absence de providence bienveillante ?<br>L'absence d'engagement ou de prise de parti bien nette chez Kenneth, l'écrivain qui n'a jamais aimé que son grand amour en Malaisie à l'exception de sa sœur ?<br>Le renoncement d'Umilta Campanati, qui finit ses jours en supérieure du couvent ?<br>La lente ascension au pouvoir de Carlo l'homme de bonne volonté, l'orphelin bien intentionné qui sublime ses appétits charnels envers l'épouse de son frère Domenico ? L'homme qui refuse de voir que l'homme est de lui-même porté au mal ? L'homme qui ne veut rien savoir de l'appétit inné des hommes pour le pouvoir sur leurs semblables ?<br>Le désintéressement, la modestie, l'abnégation de l'infortuné Tom Toomey ? <br><br>Peut-être. Mais je crois bien qu'il y a autre chose derrière toutes ces formes de pouvoir... <br><br><br><b>4. UNE TRAGÉDIE FAMILIALE</b><br><br>Earthly Powers commence au 81ème anniversaire de Kenneth M. Toomey, le narrateur de ces quelque 650 pages. Le fil rouge de son récit, s'il en faut un, c'est la faillite des familles Toomey et Campanati. <br>Monsieur et Madame Campanati, Rafaelle, Domenico, Umilta et Carlo.<br>Monsieur et Madame Toomey, Tom, Kenneth, Hortense.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[À sa première rencontre avec Kenneth, Monsieur Campanati est impotent et sénile, Madame Campanati va contracter le cancer et perdre la vie dans une tentative d'assassinat, Raffaele va faire les frais de son intransigeance face à la mafia sicilienne à Chicago ; Domenico, stérile et auteur de compositions légères, désavoué presque toute sa vie ; Umilta renonce au siècle et finit ses jours au couvent, Carlo ne connaît aucun de ses parents et se mue en Papa irascible de tous ceux qu'il rencontre. Tom connaît la maladie et la misère affective, Kenneth l'exil, Hortense ne parvient jamais à s'affranchir de ses liens familiaux et pourtant, toute sa progéniture s'éteint avant elle. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><br><b>5. LA LANGUE ET LA NARRATION</b><br><br>Ce livre est un objet étrange. <br>A peine j'ai cru cerner une progression, un ordre, une forme de narration précise (l'évolution de Kenneth en tant qu'écrivain, la vie pendant et après la guerre...) qu'une nouvelle trame se met en place : comédie d'erreurs, défauts tragiques chez tous les membres de la famille Campanati,... <br>Une progression, un ordre, dont le narrateur lui-même se méfie. Il souligne le laborieux exercice de reconstruction, d'imagination auquel se résume le livre. <br><br>Par une fiction sans arrêt soulignée, Anthony Burgess donne un poids paradoxal à son texte, qui rapporte les évènements romancés de sa vie qui est un vrai roman. La vraie narration de ce livre, je dirais bien que c'est la méfiance envers tous les discours qui prétendent réduire la réalité à leur narration. <br><br>Ce n'est pas un hasard si le livre est rempli de coupures de presses, d'annonces radiophoniques et télévisées, de conférences, de lettres. Pas un hasard si la dernière œuvre de Domenico Campanati est une adaptation de l'inadaptable Ulysses avec toutes ses formes de discours. Je crois que ce livre consacre le pouvoir du texte, met à jour le pouvoir bien réel des structures de langue sur les sociétés et leurs rites d'intégration, de passage, de mariage... (comme le font John Campanati et Laura). <br><br>D'où l'ambition universelle, <i>catholique</i> de ce livre, vraie frénésie de décrire que je rapproche (dans une moindre mesure) de celle de James Joyce dans son <b>Ulysses</b>.<br><br>D'où la prudence, la révérence et le scepticisme de Kenneth devant les mots. Son désir assumé de ne pas s'en tenir à une langue, à une narration. D'où son infatigable volonté d'attirer l'attention de son lecteur sur l'activité d'écriture et ce que ça suppose de reconstruction.<br><br>Est-ce que vous tenez un journal ? Moi oui, et ça m'est très familier. Ça me parle, cette prudence vis-à-vis de la façon dont on se rappelle les évènements passés. <br><br><br><b>6. LES LIVRES COUSINS :</b><br><br>Pour ses scènes de pari enfiévré :<br>Le Joueur - F.M. Dostoïevski<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12857.The_Gambler" title="The Gambler by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gambler</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1389044.Le_Joueur" title="Le Joueur by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Le Joueur</a><br><br>Pour la question qu'il pose sur la banalité du mal : <br>Les Bienveillantes - Jonathan Littell<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45883825.Les_Bienveillantes" title="Les Bienveillantes by Jonathan Littell" rel="nofollow noopener">Les Bienveillantes</a><br><br>Pour son étonnement face à l'étrangeté du monde :<br>The Transmigration of Timothy Archer - Philip K. Dick<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106585.The_Transmigration_of_Timothy_Archer" title="The Transmigration of Timothy Archer by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Transmigration of Timothy Archer</a><br><br>Pour son traitement nouveau d'un texte immémorial :<br>The Book of Genesis - Robert Crumb<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6371553.The_Book_of_Genesis" title="The Book of Genesis by Robert Crumb" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Genesis</a><br><br>Pour ses jeux de mots et de points de vue :<br>Exercices de Style - Raymond Queneau<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/319790.Exercises_in_Style" title="Exercises in Style by Raymond Queneau" rel="nofollow noopener">Exercises in Style</a><br><br>Pour sa plume pas sage et son plaisir de lecture pas boudé :<br>Ulysses - James Joyce<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6957527.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_1341543485" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1341543485" />
    <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool"><img alt="Jonathan Pool" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484567350p2/27950748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1341543485?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Pool" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool">Jonathan Pool</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780091439101" href="/book/show/2850909-earthly-powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=british_irish-contemporary">british_irish-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=booker-to-2015">booker-to-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=booker-listed">booker-listed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1341543485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14972368291967316705"><b> Earthly Powers</b> is a very good book. It's a long book, densely packed, and one that shifts between different eras (in the c.20th), and multiple continents.<br/>Earthly Powers is not a particularly famous or widely read work by any means, despite its Booker Prize shortlisting in 1980. The small number of Goodreads ratings is some evidence of that; A Clockwork Orange will always be the defining literary work associated with (prolific) Anthony Burgess.<br/>Twenty years after Earthly Powers, William Boyd was </span>
  <span id="freeText14972368291967316705" style="display:none"><b> Earthly Powers</b> is a very good book. It's a long book, densely packed, and one that shifts between different eras (in the c.20th), and multiple continents.<br />Earthly Powers is not a particularly famous or widely read work by any means, despite its Booker Prize shortlisting in 1980. The small number of Goodreads ratings is some evidence of that; A Clockwork Orange will always be the defining literary work associated with (prolific) Anthony Burgess.<br />Twenty years after Earthly Powers, William Boyd was Booker longlisted for Any Human Heart. There are obvious comparisons between the two books, and the two writers; Boyd is a much easier read, Burgess by far the cleverer.<br /><br />Earthly Powers is a novel structured around good and evil, incorporating certain specific historical events to illustrate the underlying philosophical debate.<br />While Earthly Powers can be read as a novel with a strong overall theme, I found it mostly enjoyable as a series of only loosely connected stories within the whole:<br /><br /><u>Nazism</u><br />A subject as extensively discussed as any other in the last seventy years. Burgess, writing thirty five years after World War Two is masterful in his demolition of the spurious 'justification' of Nazi delusions of an Aryan Race. <br /><br /><br /><u>(American) cults</u><br />The Jonestown massacre, the horrific mass suicide that happened at the back end of 1978, is undoubtedly the inspiration for the incorporation, fictionally, in Earthly Powers, of a Californian cult. Burgess also anticipates the slightly different horrors of Waco, thirteen years later, ironically the year he died.<br />Again, masterfully told.<br /><br /><u>The Catholic Church</u><br />Earthly Powers is an expression of Burgess's (lapsed) Catholic faith. Burgess questions the place of religious thought in a modern, secular world, by reference to original sin, free will, good and evil. I am no theologian, but Burgess writes most convincingly, and with respectful balance. Again, masterful.<br /><br /><u>Homosexuality</u><br />Homosexuality is, with Catholicism, a main and recurring theme. The homosexuality in Earthly Powers was a bit much for me. Not its ubiquitousness, not its moral justification, not the reality of homosexual preference, and love. The narrator's sister, Hortense, remarks, p311 "'why do you make everything sound so cold and horrid" That was my reaction at times. Earthly Powers addresses rape and paedophilia.The book was written in 1980, the year before Aids, but while attitudes towards casual sex, and responsible sex, changed after 1980, I still felt that Burgess addressed sex with too light a touch. <br />A number of reviewers have drawn attention to the opening lines of EP <i>"I was in bed with my catemite"</i>. Unlike the rapes and under age sex, prostitution is sometimes consensual I guess.<br /><br /><u>Colonial Life; the ex-patriot</u><br />Earthly Powers features a snapshot of the Malay Peninsula (a part of the world written up by Burgess in his early work, and where he lived for a time).<br />This was my favourite section in Earthly Powers. There's humour and there's warm, male friendship, and love. p250 Mahalingham describes his religion not as ‘eclectic’, but ‘electric’ and then ‘eccentric’. When queried, he retorts:<br /><i>”You don't have monopoly on language”</i>!!!<br />The friendship between Kenneth Toomey and Dr Philip Shawcross is the only relationship devoid of suspicion, of a motive, of betrayal.<br /><br /><u>Author as actor in the fictional novel</u><br />Burgess plays around with the structure of EP throughout. He frequently addresses the reader directly, and introduces numerous famous literary figures as part of the dialogue. A typical example is Burgess's fictional alter ego talking with James Joyce, and taking this further, telling Joyce in a bar about a personal encounter(!) with a real Irish figure, George Russell, fictionalised by Joyce in Ulysses. Anthony Burgess is fearsomely erudite and the intertwining reality and fiction makes for a multi layered work of fiction. Thomas Pynchon and Burgess crossed paths, and it shows in some similarities of writing style.<br /><br />Earthly Powers is a great book and Anthony Burgess is not a ‘one trick pony’ novelist that I fear may be the consequence of his renown as the author of A Clockwork Orange (to which he refers in Earthly Powers).<br /><br />I can’t wait to read some more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14972368291967316705" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_238020607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/238020607" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/238020607?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer238020607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4470895299901857629">One of my nearest and dearest sent me her old mass-market copy years ago in one of her purging moods. And, in a deeply unusual act, I've read it twice!<br/><br/>I see lots of breakdowns of this story's alleged core, the <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_of_evil' rel='nofollow noopener'>Problem of Evil</a> that besets monotheistic religions. In my own opinion, Burgess's point was less simple, as the Problem of Evil is easily resolved (you're wrong, there is no gawd so there is no problem): How, when a man is inextricably linked to another, "superior" or "better" man in the pu</span>
  <span id="freeText4470895299901857629" style="display:none">One of my nearest and dearest sent me her old mass-market copy years ago in one of her purging moods. And, in a deeply unusual act, I've read it twice!<br /><br />I see lots of breakdowns of this story's alleged core, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_of_evil" rel="nofollow noopener">Problem of Evil</a> that besets monotheistic religions. In my own opinion, Burgess's point was less simple, as the Problem of Evil is easily resolved (you're wrong, there is no gawd so there is no problem): How, when a man is inextricably linked to another, "superior" or "better" man in the public's awareness, does one contextualize the richness of either's soul in simple material terms?<br /><br />...you know, come to think of it, I can't figure out a non-spoilery way to review the book...one can't explain the power dynamic that undergirds every single decision and shapes every attitude in the men's long, untangential involvement without being either coy or obscurantist. So what can one do to discuss it? I believe my enduring puzzlement at the impossibility of writing a satisfying review has been puzzled out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4470895299901857629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph"><img alt="Paul E. Morph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602970987p2/44824883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1356717500?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul E. Morph" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph">Paul E. Morph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19098531-earthly-powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44824883-paul-e-morph?shelf=books-read-in-2019">books-read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1356717500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16999224065886047204">This chunky doorstop is an excellent novel that explores the nature of art, family, sexuality, power, authority, violence, illness, love, language and religion. Essentially, I think it's about the nature of humanity, and is very much a 'warts-and-all' delving into the subject.<br/><br/>I really enjoyed this one and was gripped all the way through. I'd give it five stars but, if I'm totally honest with myself, there were definitely moments I found... annoying.<br/><br/>Despite that; great book!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1009208296" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1009208296" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2268905-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422639392p2/2268905.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1009208296?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/2268905-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780091439101" href="/book/show/2850909-earthly-powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=gay">gay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=first-edition">first-edition</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2268905-chris?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1009208296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17624455442792119162"><b>"Sin? Such nonsense."</b><br/><br/><i>Earthly Powers</i> is a magnificent book, one of the best books I have ever read, no exaggeration. It's difficult to categorize since so many adjectives apply to it: historical, sexual, political, religious, artistic, comedic, playful, supremely literary. Most of all, it's relentlessly, uncompromisingly, unashamedly, intellectual. Thus, unfortunately it's little read today, it if ever was, and serves as no modern model—hardly a negative attribute. I don't feel such a book as <i>Ear</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17624455442792119162" style="display:none"><b>"Sin? Such nonsense."</b><br /><br /><i>Earthly Powers</i> is a magnificent book, one of the best books I have ever read, no exaggeration. It's difficult to categorize since so many adjectives apply to it: historical, sexual, political, religious, artistic, comedic, playful, supremely literary. Most of all, it's relentlessly, uncompromisingly, unashamedly, intellectual. Thus, unfortunately it's little read today, it if ever was, and serves as no modern model—hardly a negative attribute. I don't feel such a book as <i>Earthly Powers</i> needs a review—it stands apart, monumentally. Besides, I am incapable of doing it justice, so I won't try.<br /><br />As Paul Theroux writes in the book's <i>Introduction,</i> "It is such a pleasure to see such a grand edifice of intelligence, humor, ambition, and imagination, that it is impossible on reviewing it to appear less than rapturous."<br /><br />Here is Burgess' purpose for the book, as stated within by his main character, the author Ken Toomey: "I can't accept that a work of fiction should be either immoral or moral. It should merely show the world as it is and have no moral bias. It is for the reader to see in the book the nature of the motives of human actions and perhaps learn something, too, of the motives behind the social forces which judge those actions and which, I take it, we call a system of morality." (181)<br /><br />Think about that for a moment. Does it not most precisely defiine the author-reader contract? Do you hear the intellectual challenge Burgess offers to his readers? Can you just about imagine the beautiful free-thinking which Burgess displays unapologetically throughout the book?<br /><br />Well...enough! I won't write some trite, embarrassing, gushing review.<br /><br />But I will share some characteristic quotes from the book! (There are many. This list is really a reference for me years from now, so scroll down if you are uninterested.)<br /><blockquote><br />"It was the afternoon of my eighty-first birthday, and I was in bed with my catamite when Ali announced that the archbishop had come to see me." (11) [Here is one of the best opening lines in all of literature. In addition to being "arresting," it emcompassses the entire scope of the book in one sentence. This should be studied in lit classes, if there are any actually being taught.]<br /><br />"God blast and bloody well damn this bloody stinking place." (14)<br /><br />"The world was once all miracle. Then everything started to be explained. Everything will be explained in time. It's just a matter of waiting." (21)<br /><br />"As for the Nobel, I did not write inelegantly or tendentiously enough." (25)<br /><br />"I liked Jim Joyce but not his demented experiments with language. He threw away the chance of becoming a great novelist in the great tradition of Stendhal. He was always trying to make literature a substitute for religion." (74)<br /><br />"My capacity for love was hedged in by all the thundering edicts of Moses." (77)<br /><br />"Sin? Such nonsense." (86)<br /><br />"I expected a little gift, you know, something nice and useless, you know, from Cartier's." (144)<br /><br />"States and Churches alike must forbid pleasure. Pleasure renders the partaker indifferent to the power of both." (188)<br /><br />[!!!] "And the boy he took his lover like a beast, thrusting his empurpled royal greatness into the antrum, without tenderness, with no cooings of love, rather with grunts and howls, his unpaired nails drawing blood from breast and belly, and the sky opened for both of them, disclosing in blinding radiance the lineaments of a benedicent numen." [!!!] (191)<br /><br />"Joe Conrad's sea smells of Roget's thesaurus, as I was always telling him, but he wouldn't listen." (192)<br /><br />"Stertile thunder tonitruated terribly. 'Oh Lord forgive us our bloody sins.' Rain now pelted. It was hard work finding a taxi." (199) [Joycean? Nabokovian? Yes.]<br /><br />"Everyone has a right to be born. No one has a right to live." (226) [Oh I heartily disagree with this, but the fictional future Pope said it. It's a concise distillation of, shall we say, a serious problem with a certain religion.]<br /><br />"Once the Christians fought the Moslems, and then the Christians fought each other. Faith is hard to sustain unless it is either beleaguered or dreams the imperial dream." (231)<br /><br />"This postwar world's learning to separate the act of sex from the act of generation. The Church says that's a sin. But it's deliberately chosen, a healthy act of free will. If it's a sin then I'm predisposed to sin. The Church and I can't agree on it. So I'm out of the Church. Very simple and very unfair." (306)<br /><br />"Religion is the most dangerous thing in the world. It is not little girls in their communion frocks and silly holy pictures and the Children of Mary. It is highly explosive, dynamite, the splitting of the atom." (349)<br /><br />"These are bad bad times. This is the worst century that history has ever known. And we're only a third of the way through it. There have to be martyrs and witnesses." (381)<br /><br />"And what were you doing in Paris?" "Seeing James Joyce. The Irish writer. A confirmed neutral in the last war, despite his British passport. Trained by Jesuits. Author of <i>Ulysses,</i> long banned for dirtiness. He'd promised me a copy of <i>Finnegans Wake.</i> Signed. A great experimental masterpiece. Confiscated by HM Customs for investigation. I assured them it was not in code. Damn it all, the publishers are Faber and Faber." (435)<br /><br />"Black is no colour, merely a brutal politicoracist abstraction, and it was the texture of her skin that struck before it's indefinable hue, or rather was inseparable from it, the pleasure of the sight of it only, one knew, to be completed by the most delicate palpation: as if honey and satin were one substance and both alive and yet sculpted of richest gold." (474)<br /><br />"Meanwhile in France a new breed of writers was producing the <i>nouveau roman,</i> based on the rejection of plot and character and, indeed, everything I have always stood for. It was perhaps with unspoken relief that, admiring these, professors of fiction took my own works to bed and, enjoying them, had to rationalize their enjoyment in terms of my consciously, in a kind of revolt against postmodernism, ridiculous term, reverting to an earlier tradition. I was not, of course, reverting at all." (523)<br /><br />"Homosexuals may be in the minority, your honor, though I submit that there is less thoroughgoing heterosexuality in the community than orthodoxy would have us believe. Nevertheless, homosexuals have a right to an expression of their own view of life and love. Our literature has been grievously harmed by the suppression of that right. So, God help us, has society in general. No man or woman can help being homosexual. I cannot help it myself." (530)<br /><br />"What the hell do you mean, real father? There are no real fathers, only legal ones. Mothers are different, mothers are all too real." (587)<br /><br />"History has been unfair to Socrates. Just as it's probably been unfair to Christ. History is too often written by heterosexuals." (598)<br /></blockquote><br />If these quotes aren't enough, as evidence, perhaps, of the literary heft of <i>Earthly Powers,</i> I created an index to the many authors mentioned in the book. Burgess uses them both as elements of plot and to bring twentieth-century ideas into the novel as it evolves historically. A few of these are actual characters—James Joyce, for example, with whom Toomey has several fascinating and funny conversations full of word-play.<br /><br />I did this as an amusement while reading, never myself too bothered by justifiable, substantive name-dropping. The page numbers are first instances only (several appear repeatedly) and are in textual sequence. There are also many invented authors who serve Burgess' critical needs. These are not listed here, unless I failed with the reality check.<br /><br />Henry James (12), Rainer Maria Rilke (13), Norman Douglas (19), Thomas Campion (20), Thomas More (20), Aldous Huxley (21), Walt Whitman (22), W. Somerset Maugham (25), Hermann Hesse (37), Ernest Hemingway (49), Samuel Butler (50), James Joyce (50), Rupert Brooke (59), F. Scott Fitzgerald (63), Omar Khayyam (63), Edward Thomas (64), Ezra Pound (68), George Bernard Shaw (69), Joris-Karl Huysmans (69), Oscar Wilde (69), W. W. Jacobs (76), P. G. Wodehouse (76), Gustave Flaubert (76), Honoré de Balzac (76), Victor Hugo (76), Compton Mckenzie (78), Hugh Walpole (78), D. H. Lawrence (78), Graham Greene (79), H. G. Wells (79), Eden Philpotts (80), Arnold Bennett (80), Max Beerbohm (81), Moliére (87), E. M. Forster (104), Edmond Rostand (117), William Shakespeare (117), Blaise Pascal (123), W. H. Auden (126), Christopher Isherwood (126), Robert Browning (126), Havelock Ellis (160), T. S. Eliot (161), Sigmund Freud (161), Radcliffe Hall (163), Ford Madox Ford (165), Joseph Conrad (192), Ezra Pound (192), Sylvia Beach (192), Adrienne Monnier (192), Hall Caine (193), Valery Larbaud (194), Wyndham Lewis (198), Oswald Mosley (209), Rudyard Kipling (209), John Milton (214), Marcel Proust (216), André Gide (223), George Eliot (257), Edgar Wallace (268), François Rabelais (289), Gertrude Stein (310), Edward Spenser (322), E. E. Cummings (339), Geoffrey of Monmouth (345), Stefan Zweig (420), John Middleton Murry (423), Kate Mansfield (423), George Orwell (424), Marie Corelli (523), William Thackeray (529), Charles Dickens (529), Henry Miller (529), John Donne (529), Richard Crashaw (529), Jeremy Taylor (529), Christopher Marlowe (530), Anatole France (535), Nevil Shute (572), J. D. Salinger (573), Virgil (573), James Baldwin (576), Ralph Ellison (576), Isaac Bashevis Singer (582), Gerard Manley Hopkins (602), Frederick "Fr" Rolfe (622)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17624455442792119162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_172844465" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172844465" />
    <a title="Carol Storm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5032322-carol-storm"><img alt="Carol Storm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442507712p2/5032322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172844465?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Storm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Storm" href="/user/show/5032322-carol-storm">Carol Storm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671414900" href="/book/show/1293794.Earthly_Powers">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172844465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8473510310688848856">Just as Bela Lugosi will forever be known as Dracula, and Boris Karloff as Frankenstein's Monster, so Anthony Burgess will forever be known as the author of A CLOCKWORK ORANGE. Alex casts a long shadow!<br/><br/>Nevertheless, take this book on its own terms and read honestly, and you will find that by and large it stinks on its own merits. Burgess has a sense of humor and can talk entertainingly about literature, history, and religion. But that's about it. Emotionally this book is a galactic void. Toomey </span>
  <span id="freeText8473510310688848856" style="display:none">Just as Bela Lugosi will forever be known as Dracula, and Boris Karloff as Frankenstein's Monster, so Anthony Burgess will forever be known as the author of A CLOCKWORK ORANGE. Alex casts a long shadow!<br /><br />Nevertheless, take this book on its own terms and read honestly, and you will find that by and large it stinks on its own merits. Burgess has a sense of humor and can talk entertainingly about literature, history, and religion. But that's about it. Emotionally this book is a galactic void. Toomey is a non-character with no real emotions, needs, values, aspirations, or even resentments. He's a homosexual before political correctness who regards his own sexual preference with disgust and dismisses all of his lovers with a sneer -- except one young doctor who dies conveniently before they can get it on. <br /><br />Mostly this book just drifts from one wisecrack to the next. To the extent that Burgess takes anything seriously he seems to be writing some sort of extended tribute to the Catholic church. But this is propaganda, not art. Burgess never once tackles the horrendous failures of the church, in the ancient world, the medieval world, or in modern times. He has no interest in the Crusades, the Inquisition, or the African slave trade. He pretends to be appalled by the crimes of the Nazis, but takes plenty of cheap shots at Jews in Hollywood, in the theater, in the literary world, and so on. He has nothing to say about Catholic anti-Semitism, or racism, or the church's twisted attitude towards sex or towards the female sex. He fudges the record on every occasion, showing his priest hero "defying" the Nazis when both the Church leadership and the vast majority of Catholics in Europe were either apathetic or openly sided with the Nazis. And needless to say, for all the hero's disgust with homosexuality, he never so much as hints at the existence of sexual deviants within the Church itself.<br /><br />EARTHLY POWERS is a big, sloppy book by a man who knows he's lying and knows he can get away with it. Because his friends are a lot more powerful than his enemies. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8473510310688848856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4277032-d?shelf=english">english</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1955935344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1216642000007145212">I immensely enjoyed reading this book. It being the first book I've ever read by this intriguing author, I'm looking forward to read his other works. The incredibly rich 'autobiographical' story is cunningly interwoven with real events in the 1900 - 1970's. Most memorably, the author becomes the brother in law of 'Carlo', who was already sure in the 1920's to become pope, and eventually did so as John XXIII. After the first world war, the 'author' flees England to escape prosecution as a practic</span>
  <span id="freeText1216642000007145212" style="display:none">I immensely enjoyed reading this book. It being the first book I've ever read by this intriguing author, I'm looking forward to read his other works. The incredibly rich 'autobiographical' story is cunningly interwoven with real events in the 1900 - 1970's. Most memorably, the author becomes the brother in law of 'Carlo', who was already sure in the 1920's to become pope, and eventually did so as John XXIII. After the first world war, the 'author' flees England to escape prosecution as a practicing homosexual and becomes successful while living in interesting places like Monaco, New York, Hollywood, Tangier, Barcelona (under Franco!) and Malta. <br /><br />You learn a lot from reading this, at least I did. Mostly about church history, e.g. the arian heresy and much, much more. This is because the author was raised catholic and, while 'leaving' the church because of his homosexuality, never really gave up on it. <br /><br />Also, his vocabulary is stunning, at least to me. I had to look up quite a few words, e.g. 'redolence', 'inchoate', 'strabismus venerean', and 'etoliated'. I suspect even native speakers might have trouble with some of those.<br /><br />The only negative point is that towards the end, the story seems to stall a bit and previous patterns are repeated. So, in my opinion, the book could be a tiny bit shorter to make it perfect.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1216642000007145212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_152332460" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152332460?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyle" href="/user/show/4263667-kyle">Kyle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671414900" href="/book/show/1293794.Earthly_Powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4263667-kyle?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4263667-kyle?shelf=religion">religion</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152332460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5973945277690523847">This is a hell of a book.<br/><br/>It took me about two and a half months to read, even though it's not one of the longest books I've read. That's cause this sucker is DENSE - no book for someone looking for an easy read.<br/><br/>The narrator, Kenneth Toomey, is a British novelist, now in his eighties, looking back over his life. Despite the fact that he is openly homosexual, officials from the Catholic Church want him to write for them - an account about the recently deceased pope, Gregory XVII, or Carlo Campanat</span>
  <span id="freeText5973945277690523847" style="display:none">This is a hell of a book.<br /><br />It took me about two and a half months to read, even though it's not one of the longest books I've read. That's cause this sucker is DENSE - no book for someone looking for an easy read.<br /><br />The narrator, Kenneth Toomey, is a British novelist, now in his eighties, looking back over his life. Despite the fact that he is openly homosexual, officials from the Catholic Church want him to write for them - an account about the recently deceased pope, Gregory XVII, or Carlo Campanati. The two men have lived fairly entwined lives - Ken's sister marries Carlo's brother, and they become a sort of family. <br /><br />Both Toomey and Campanati are brilliantly realized characters. The arch, snooty voice of Toomey sells the whole book, as he relates the stories of his fame and notoriety. But Carlo is a mystery of sorts, a solid man who believes that evil is an outside force, that man is basically good. But does Toomey share that view? Not quite...<br /><br />I was hoping this wasn't one of those "here's how my characters live through the various incidents of a historical period" novels, and it wasn't. Though Toomey and Campanati encounter Italian fascists, Nazi propogandists, and groovy Californian cult leaders, the characters never take a back seat to events - the events inform us more about the characters. I really appreciated that.<br /><br />All I've known of Burgess is his (admittedly impressive) Clockwork Orange, but after Earthly Powers my interest is piqued. Definitely one of the most unique and memorable books I've read this year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5973945277690523847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44569771" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44569771" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44569771?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671414900" href="/book/show/1293794.Earthly_Powers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44569771" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10906657115761460803">"It was the afternoon of my eighty-first birthday, and I was in bed with my catamite when Ali announced that the archbishop had come to see me." <br/><br/>Earthly Powers is the linchpin of Anthony Burgess' novel-writing career. It is a massive work that compares favorably with similar tomes of twentieth century literature. What sets Burgess apart from other authors is his linguistic playfulness combined with an exceptional narrative style. Although this style is here somewhat less obviously experimental t</span>
  <span id="freeText10906657115761460803" style="display:none">"It was the afternoon of my eighty-first birthday, and I was in bed with my catamite when Ali announced that the archbishop had come to see me." <br /><br />Earthly Powers is the linchpin of Anthony Burgess' novel-writing career. It is a massive work that compares favorably with similar tomes of twentieth century literature. What sets Burgess apart from other authors is his linguistic playfulness combined with an exceptional narrative style. Although this style is here somewhat less obviously experimental than that of Burgess’s other novels of this period, his use of a professional story teller as a first-person narrator allows him to call into the question the nature of authority in fictional texts. The narrative becomes a retrospective account of a life spent as an outsider. Within that account, Burgess locates his protagonist,Toomey, at some key moments of twentieth century history in order, it seems, to comment on those issues which consistently surface in all of Burgess’s fiction, particularly the nature of evil and its presence in the physical world. The novel attempts to address issues of belief, and the role of religion in late twentieth century culture, using a broad cast of characters, fictional and real; it is not, however, a roman à clef. Though often mentioned in reviews of this novel, the identification of Toomey with Somerset Maugham fails to recognise that Toomey is a portmanteau of many characters. He contains hints of Maugham, certainly, but there are suggestions of, to name a few, Alec Waugh in the precocious young novelist; of P. G. Wodehouse in the broadcaster from Berlin; of W. H. Auden in the rescuer of a Nobel laureate’s offspring; and of Burgess himself, the author of a real Blooms of Dublin. Burgess ability to meld this amalgam of characters into his protagonist reminds me of another favorite novel, The New Confessions by William Boyd, in which the author uses a similar technique to create a tremendously exciting and interesting protagonist. Throughout the novel, the emphasis is on the debate about the nature of evil rather than on the accuracy or otherwise of the references to twentieth century figures. The novel examines at length the nature of belief, the way in which people cope with an imperfect world, and the operation of evil and suffering. In doing so it succeeds in presenting a distinctive and compelling view of the twentieth century through the life of Toomey. It is both a challenging and rewarding read that I would recommend to all.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10906657115761460803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
